<div id="review_1363242779" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1363242779" />
    <a title="Lexie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20726801-lexie"><img alt="Lexie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524678402p2/20726801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1363242779?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lexie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lexie" href="/user/show/20726801-lexie">Lexie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783150091852" href="/book/show/4109.Rosencrantz_and_Guildenstern_are_Dead">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20726801-lexie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20726801-lexie?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1363242779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16042306584086588789">I never feel fully qualified to review books of this caliber with any hope to encompass everything they stand for. But Rosencrantz and Guildenstern is approachable and easy <s>and terribly difficult to spell</s>, as far as a book can be classified as approachable. And I feel like saying a few words on it won't result in a lynch mob.<br/><br/>When we are taught to look at a known scene with fresh eyes / from a fresh perspective in Creative Writing classes, it's commonplace to immediately leap to inanimate objects</span>
  <span id="freeText16042306584086588789" style="display:none">I never feel fully qualified to review books of this caliber with any hope to encompass everything they stand for. But Rosencrantz and Guildenstern is approachable and easy <s>and terribly difficult to spell</s>, as far as a book can be classified as approachable. And I feel like saying a few words on it won't result in a lynch mob.<br /><br />When we are taught to look at a known scene with fresh eyes / from a fresh perspective in Creative Writing classes, it's commonplace to immediately leap to inanimate objects and personify abstract concepts. This <i>out of the box</i> approach to creativity has become so thoroughly canvassed that it fits into the box quite nicely now. But maybe, just maybe, Rosencrantz and Guildenstern Are Dead is the end result one actually seeks in those exercises - a novel approach to a known plot, and one which brings quite a bit of new to the old. Part of this novelty that it brings obviously has to do with it having been written in 1966. The influences in this book are decidedly modern, and the writing doesn't bother to hide it. But the other part of the novelty might just lie in the reimagining - in bringing secondary characters to the forefront and having their ruminations and their (very good) jokes narrate the entire story.<br /><br />Having said all this, I did feel at times that the book trod a dangerous line between being <i>reminiscent</i> of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17716.Waiting_for_Godot" title="Waiting for Godot by Samuel Beckett" rel="nofollow noopener">Waiting for Godot</a> and of <i>being</i> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17716.Waiting_for_Godot" title="Waiting for Godot by Samuel Beckett" rel="nofollow noopener">Waiting for Godot</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16042306584086588789" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33723950?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeeyun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeeyun" href="/user/show/93831-jeeyun">Jeeyun</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33723950" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4011497533627676244">This re-read could not have come at a better time deep in the midst of existential crisis #522. This is the clever tragicomic meta-play of two minor characters from Shakespeare's Hamlet. They are summoned, they bumble about, they play questions, they are entirely confounded by the hubbub surrounding the "much transformed" Prince of Denmark, and then they are sent to their feeble deaths as demanded by the grand scheme of the play. While the dialogue and physical antics of Rosencrantz, Guildenster</span>
  <span id="freeText4011497533627676244" style="display:none">This re-read could not have come at a better time deep in the midst of existential crisis #522. This is the clever tragicomic meta-play of two minor characters from Shakespeare's Hamlet. They are summoned, they bumble about, they play questions, they are entirely confounded by the hubbub surrounding the "much transformed" Prince of Denmark, and then they are sent to their feeble deaths as demanded by the grand scheme of the play. While the dialogue and physical antics of Rosencrantz, Guildenstern, and the Tragediens are comical and laden with puns, the play serves as a philosophical musing on life, the futility of it, and the cruel haphazard nature of creation (and death). But... um... all in good fun of course. <br /><br />I can't imagine this being particularly interesting reading if you haven't read Hamlet or if you don't enjoy pointless witticisms and humor in the absurd. <br /><br />The play always leaves me thinking that maybe we're all just minor characters summoned from nothing to pass the time idly waxing philosophic and idiotic while we wait to fulfill our minor role after which we make our sad, pathetic exit without applause or encore. But... um... in a fun(ny) way, I promise you...</span>
  <a data-text-id="4011497533627676244" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_665373801" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/665373801" />
    <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata"><img alt="Debbie Zapata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451631863p2/22110057.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer13915043604580066053">My tummy hurts from laughing so much. Reading this play is even more fun if you have seen the movie version a dozen or so times and can have it running through your mind as you turn the pages of the book.<br/><br/>Rosencrantz and Guildenstern have become my favorite characters in Hamlet because of this play. You just have to root for them, even when you know what their fate will be. They try so hard to understand what is happening but they can never quite grasp enough details for their lives to make sense</span>
  <span id="freeText13915043604580066053" style="display:none">My tummy hurts from laughing so much. Reading this play is even more fun if you have seen the movie version a dozen or so times and can have it running through your mind as you turn the pages of the book.<br /><br />Rosencrantz and Guildenstern have become my favorite characters in Hamlet because of this play. You just have to root for them, even when you know what their fate will be. They try so hard to understand what is happening but they can never quite grasp enough details for their lives to make sense. <br /><br />I want to go right back to page one and start all over again. I think I will toss a coin: heads, I re-read immediately, tails I go on to something else.......Heads! </span>

<div id="review_784661410" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/784661410" />
    <a title="Margaret" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10716214-margaret"><img alt="Margaret" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554877534p2/10716214.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/784661410?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret" href="/user/show/10716214-margaret">Margaret</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10716214-margaret?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10716214-margaret?shelf=poetry-and-plays">poetry-and-plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10716214-margaret?shelf=2013-challenge">2013-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10716214-margaret?shelf=english-classics">english-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer784661410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17407647504069892658">Having read the play I am now totally enchanted by Rosencrantz and Guilderstern are Dead.<br/><br/>Rosecrantz And Guildenstern Are Dead follows two minor characters from "Hamlet" as all the action from that play sweeps around them. It is absurd, funny, sad and poignant by turns.<br/><br/>Sir Tom Stoppard is quite possibly the greatest playwright of the 20th Century.<br/><br/>I can't wait to watch the filmed version that I got from the library.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5Bcysua0B4/bHtYcMNQjUj1zZSpaYx8iyvgYqkfKcbbolFYzqPiJJVDWIKKrWcluZEXiELoRciWJpOJ/XjbDVg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EL1kjgdg3TMCo4j9nSMIqbbpOdWBla793PZAYpKk5mAcPgAPSSxTmYlrfkMGruKV79++72Hnw/qfqrq3i1hUgA==')})
  }
//]]>

The rapid-fire wit and eccentric characters of The Importance of Being Earnest have made it a mainstay of the high school curriculum for decades.\n\nCecily Cardew and Gwendolen Fairfax are both in love with the same mythical suitor. Jack Worthing has wooed Gwendolen as Ernest while Algernon has also posed as Ernest to win the heart of Jack\'s ward, Cecily. When all four arrive at Jack\'s country home on the same weekend the &quot;rivals&quot; to fight for Ernest\'s undivided attention and the &quot;Ernests&quot; to claim their beloveds pandemonium breaks loose. Only a senile nursemaid and an old, discarded hand-bag can save the day!\n\nThis Prestwick House Literary Touchstone Edition includes a glossary and reader\'s notes to help the modern reader The play also daringly showcased the burden of sexuality repressed in the agony of her husband, Brick Pollitt. In spite of the public controversy Cat stirred up, it was awarded the Pulitzer Prize and the Drama Critics Circle Award for that year. Williams, as he so often did with his plays, rewrote Cat on a Hot Tin Roof for many years—the present version was originally produced at the American Shakespeare Festival in 1974 with all the changes that made Williams finally declare the text to be definitive, and was most recently produced on Broadway in the 2003–2004 season. This definitive edition also includes Williams&amp;rsquoi; essay “Person-to-Person,” Williams’ notes on the various endings, and a short chronology of the author’s life. One of America’s greatest living playwrights, as well as a friend and colleague of Williams, Edward Albee has written a concise introduction to the play from a playwright’s perspective, examining the
